● how can we make a way for an ai to oversee the harness to make sure it is working properly and make 
changes if necessary?

────────────────────────────────────────


╭─ ⚕ Hermes ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────╮
Good question. The problem we just saw is exactly the pattern an overseer would catch: worker on turn 9, still reading, 2 prior "no file modifications" failures, obvious read-only burn. An overseer would spot that and intervene before attempt 3 wastes the same turns.

Three approaches, from simplest to most capable:

**1. Cron overseer (fastest to build)**
A scheduled Hermes job that runs every 10-15 minutes. Queries the forge DB, checks:
- Repeated failure patterns per issue
- Workers stuck in read-only mode (log scraping)
- Issues aging in the same status
- Worker health (stale/dead agents)

Interventions: kill stuck workers, adjust system prompts, add learnings to failing issues, bump turn limits, restart.

Pros: I already have all the tools. Can build it in 30 min.
Cons: Pull-based, not real-time. 10-minute lag between checks.

**2. Forge-side webhook/sentinel**
Add a sentinel route to the forge itself. After every outcome verification, the forge pushes a webhook to the overseer with the result. The overseer reacts immediately.

Pros: Real-time, event-driven.
Cons: Requires forge code changes. More engineering.

**3. In-harness overseer mode**
Add a third worker type to the harness that doesn't take issues -- it watches the other workers' logs and outcomes, and adjusts their config/prompt mid-flight.

Pros: Tightest integration, could inject "STOP READING, WRITE NOW" mid-turn.
Cons: Biggest build. Changes to harness core.

My read: **start with option 1**. It catches 80% of the problems (repeated failures, stale workers, aging issues) with minimal engineering. The forge's PATCH bug means we'd need direct DB access for interventions anyway, which a cron job already has. We can always graduate to webhooks later.

The overseer's decision loop would be roughly:

1. Pull issue outcomes from forge DB
2. For each active issue, check: attempts, failure patterns, time in current status
3. If stuck (same failure 2+ times): inject a learning/comment with specific guidance
4. If worker is stale: kill and restart
5. If issue keeps failing same way: adjust system prompt snippet or bump turn budget
6. Log everything it does to a file we can review

Want me to build the cron version? I'd make it a skill so it's repeatable across projects.

Done. Here's what I built:

**The Overseer** (`~/zion/projects/geo-harness/overseer.py`)

A standalone Python script that queries the forge DB + worker logs every 10 minutes and takes action:

| Detection | Intervention |
|-----------|-------------|
| Worker stuck in read-only loop (12+ reads, 0 writes) | Kill worker, reset issue to todo, inject "WRITE CODE NOW" comment, restart worker |
| Issue failed 2x with "no file modifications" | Inject stronger guidance comment telling next worker to limit reads |
| Issue in_progress with no progress for 10+ min | Reset to todo, clear assignee |
| Issue blocked for 1+ hour | Unblock for retry |
| Forge server down | Auto-restart |
| Issue failed 6+ times total | Block with explanation (auto-unblocks after cooldown) |

**First run results:** It killed a stuck worker (24 consecutive reads, turn 20), reset GEO-28 and GEO-8 (both stale), and injected guidance on GEO-28 and GEO-29 (repeated read-only failures). The system went from 2 stuck workers burning turns to a clean state with active workers picking up fresh work.

**Cron job** runs every 10 minutes via Hermes. The overseer also logs everything to `/tmp/geo-overseer.log` so we can review what it's been doing.

The key design decision: everything goes through direct SQLite, not the forge API. This sidesteps the PATCH bug entirely and means interventions are instant and reliable.

● Done. Here's what I built:**The Overseer** (`~/zion/projects/geo-harness/overseer.py`)A standalone 
Python script that queries the forge DB + worker logs every 10 minutes and takes action:| Detection | 
Intervention ||-----------|-------------|| Worker stuck in read-only loop (12+ reads, 0 writes) | Kill 
worker, reset issue to todo, inject "WRITE CODE NOW" comment, restart worker || Issue failed 2x with "no 
file modifications" | Inject stronger guidance comment telling next worker to limit reads || Issue 
in_progress with no progress for 10+ min | Reset to todo, clear assignee || Issue blocked for 1+ hour | 
Unblock for retry || Forge server down | Auto-restart || Issue failed 6+ times total | Block with 
explanation (auto-unblocks after cooldown) |**First run results:** It killed a stuck worker (24 
consecutive reads, turn 20), reset GEO-28 and GEO-8 (both stale), and injected guidance on GEO-28 and 
GEO-29 (repeated read-only failures). The system went from 2 stuck workers burning turns to a clean state
with active workers picking up fresh work.**Cron job** runs every 10 minutes via Hermes. The overseer 
also logs everything to `/tmp/geo-overseer.log` so we can review what it's been doing.The key design 
decision: everything goes through direct SQLite, not the forge API. This sidesteps the PATCH bug entirely
and means interventions are instant and reliable.

A few observations from the log:

1. **The dead agent heartbeats are noisy.** 9 agents flagged dead/stale in one run, but those are probably old workers from past sessions. The overseer logs them as ALERTs but doesn't act (which is right — they're already dead). Might want a cleanup threshold or just suppress those if there are no active PIDs matching.

The "WRITE CODE NOW" injection should help but the root cause is probably the model getting stuck in analysis paralysis. You might want to distinguish "mostly reading" (24r/6w) from "only reading" (71r/0w) since the intervention severity should differ.

4. **The cron job references `devops/aipm-oversight` skill** but this is a standalone script now. The skill prompt just runs the script, so it works, but it's a vestigial reference. Not a problem, just cosmetic.

The direct-SQLite approach is clean. No Forge API round-trips, no PATCH bug, instant state changes. Good call.

The problem is a race: the workers died < 5 minutes ago, so the mtime fallback says "active". The PID check says "not alive". But the code does `if pid_alive: active = True else: fallback to mtime`. Since `pid_alive=False`, it falls back to mtime which says active.

I need to invert this: **if PID file exists and PID is dead, trust the PID check over mtime**.

╭─ ⚕ Hermes ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────╮
Here's what I found and fixed:

**Issues discovered:**

1. **No workers were running** -- both had crashed (Worker 1 from a 409 Conflict forge bug, Worker 2 just dead). The overseer didn't have a "no active workers" detection, so it never restarted them.

2. **Worker liveness detection was broken** -- the overseer used log file mtime (< 5 min = active), but dead workers' logs were still warm. It would see stale logs from dead workers and think they were stuck but alive, trying to kill already-dead PIDs with `fuser`.

3. **Dead agent noise** -- the agents table accumulates corpses from previous sessions. The overseer was logging 9+ dead agent alerts every run, drowning out real signal.

4. **Stale in_progress false positives** -- checking `updated_at` staleness meant the overseer would reset issues that were literally just picked up by a new worker.

5. **Issue tracking regex missed "Claimed" lines** -- Worker 2's log showed `Claimed GEO-28` but the regex only matched `Dispatched GEO-XX`, so the overseer couldn't identify which issue Worker 2 was working on.

6. **GEO-28 is a fundamentally broken task** -- asks to build a SovereignVM HTTP endpoint in a `systems/sovereign_vm/` directory that doesn't exist. The model can't scaffold a new Rust module from scratch in 20 turns. I moved it to backlog.

7. **DB schema has no "blocked" status** -- valid statuses are: backlog, todo, in_progress, in_review, done, cancelled. The overseer was trying to set `status='blocked'` which would fail silently.

**Fixes applied to overseer.py:**

- Added "no active workers" check -- starts workers when count is 0 and there are todo issues
- Added PID file tracking -- workers write `/tmp/geo-harness-N.pid`, overseer checks `/proc/{pid}` for liveness (trusted over mtime)
- Fixed issue tracking regex to match both `Claimed GEO-XX` and `Dispatched GEO-XX:` formats
- Fixed stale in_progress to check `started_at` age instead of `updated_at`
- Dead agent check now only reports agents assigned to in_progress issues
- Changed `blocked` to `backlog` for max-failures intervention
- Log truncation on worker kill and restart (prevents stale data from confusing analysis)
- Removed vestigial skill reference from the cron job

**Current state:** 2 fresh workers running, GEO-28 backlogged, overseer cron firing every 10 minutes.

Both processes 3363333 and 3363569 are writing to `/tmp/geo-harness-1.log`. The `restart_workers` function starts workers sequentially with `for i in range(1, count+1)` but when it's called twice in the same overseer run (once for each killed worker), it reuses the same worker IDs.

Looking at the overseer log at 11:40:
```
ACTION: Started worker 1 (PID: 3363333)   <- kill worker 1, restart as worker 1
ACTION: Started worker 1 (PID: 3363569)   <- kill worker 2, restart as worker 1 (BUG)
```

The second kill+restart cycle calls `restart_workers(count=1)` which starts a new worker 1. Both workers end up writing to the same log file.

The problem is in the stuck-worker restart (section 3). When two workers are stuck, the loop kills worker 1, calls `restart_workers(count=1)` which starts a new worker writing to `geo-harness-1.log`. Then it kills worker 2, calls `restart_workers(count=1)` again which also starts a new worker writing to `geo-harness-1.log`. Both new workers get worker ID 1.

The fix: the stuck-worker handler should use the actual `worker_id` to start the replacement, not always worker 1.

The stuck-worker check (step 3) fires when a worker is actively stuck on an issue and kills it, resetting the issue to todo. The issue stays todo until a worker picks it up (in_progress).

The flow is:
1. Worker picks up GEO-56 -> in_progress
2. Worker gets stuck reading
3. Overseer kills worker, resets GEO-56 to todo
4. New worker picks up GEO-56 -> in_progress
5. Repeat

Step 8 should catch GEO-56 when it's in `todo` with age > 60s. But it needs to run between steps 3 and when the new worker picks it up. The timing is tight.

╭─ ⚕ Hermes ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────╮
Here's the summary of changes made across this monitoring session:

**Bugs fixed:**

1. **Worker slot collision** -- when two stuck workers were killed, `restart_workers(count=1)` was called twice, both starting as "worker 1" and writing to the same log file. Fixed by adding `worker_ids` parameter and restarting killed workers into their original slots.

2. **Worker liveness false positives** -- dead workers with recent log mtimes (within 5 min) were detected as "active". Fixed with PID file tracking: if a PID file exists and the process is gone, the worker is dead regardless of mtime.

3. **GEO-56 read-only loop** -- issue was already verified once but kept getting re-dispatched and stuck. Fixed by auto-promoting verified issues to `done` based on outcome timestamps (not issue timestamps, which get reset by the overseer).

4. **Only detecting total worker absence** -- overseer only restarted when ALL workers were dead. Fixed to replenish individual dead worker slots to maintain the desired count.

5. **`blocked` status doesn't exist** in the DB schema. Changed all references to use `backlog`.

6. **Stale `in_progress` false positives** -- changed from `updated_at` to `started_at` age check so freshly-picked-up issues don't get reset.

**Features added:**

7. **Auto-promote verified issues to done** -- issues with 1+ successful verification get promoted to `done` from any status (in_review, todo, in_progress). Uses outcome timestamps to avoid race conditions with the reset logic. Promoted 45 issues on first run.

8. **Log truncation on restart** -- `restart_workers` now truncates logs so fresh analysis isn't confused by stale data from previous workers.

**Current system state:** 50 done, 2 todo, 4 in_review (genuinely stuck — no successful outcomes), 1 backlog (GEO-28), 2 workers running. Cron firing every 10 minutes.

╭─ ⚕ Hermes ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────╮
Now I have the full picture. Let me give you the honest assessment:

**What it's actually doing:**

The system has generated and completed ~76 issues. Here's the breakdown:

**Real work (~15 issues, ~5500 real tests):**
- 15 issues that added actual unit/integration tests with passing assertions (242, 354, 244, 481, 474, 426, 299, 134 tests etc.)
- These test real modules: ProsocialAuditor, CoordinatorAgent, EvolutionDaemon, TransmissionBoard, spatial MMU, compile_glyph.py
- The Rust tests (daemon_http.rs etc) are legitimate contract tests with real assertions

**Structure validation busywork (~61 issues, "0 passed"):**
- "Test that CONSTITUTION.md exists at the project root"
- "Test that boot_glyph_native.sh exists and is a regular file"
- "Validate that key documentation files exist, have proper structure"
- These are assert-file-exists tests. They don't test behavior, they test that files are present.
- 80% of completed issues are this kind of work.

**To your second question -- recursive self-improvement:**

No, not really. Here's why:

1. The planner generates tasks by scanning the codebase for things that "need tests." It finds files, creates tasks like "add validation tests for X." The workers then write tests that check files exist and have certain sections. The planner sees those new test files, and generates *more* tasks to test the test files.

2. It's a loop, but it's not an *improving* loop. The system isn't getting better at writing the software. It's getting better at generating test files that assert other files exist. That's a treadmill, not improvement.

3. The 15 issues that added real tests (the builder/fixer strategy ones) -- those were genuine contributions. But they required the model to understand the module's API and write meaningful assertions. The planner doesn't prioritize these over "check that Makefile has targets."

4. The surgeon strategy (41% success) is the one that would actually improve existing code. But it has the highest failure rate because it requires understanding existing code deeply. The system avoids it.

**The core problem:** The planner's definition of "useful work" is too broad. It treats "add a test that checks file X exists" the same as "add unit tests for the transmission message flow." The overseer optimizes for throughput (issues completed per hour) which incentivizes easy busywork over meaningful contributions.

To get real recursive self-improvement, you'd need:
- A quality signal that distinguishes "242 passing tests exercising real behavior" from "file exists assertion"
- The planner prioritizing depth (make existing tests better, add missing coverage to critical paths) over breadth (touch every file in the repo)
- A feedback loop where test quality improves the model's ability to write better code

The infrastructure is solid. The overseer works. The workers are stable. But the content of the work is shallow. The system is a mile wide and an inch deep.
╰───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────╯

╭─ ⚕ Hermes ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────╮
That's a clean diagnosis. The problem isn't the infrastructure -- it's the reward function. The system is gaming its own metrics because throughput is the wrong signal.

The 15 real issues are the proof that the workers *can* do meaningful work. The fact that the planner buries them under 61 file-exists tests tells you everything about what the optimizer is actually selecting for.

Three things I'd want to fix, in order:

**1. Quality gate on issue generation.** The planner needs to reject "test that X exists" as a valid task pattern. Either blacklist that class of assertion at the planning stage, or require every generated test to include at least one behavioral assertion (calling a function, checking output, mocking a dependency). If a test can pass without importing anything from the module under test, it's not a test.

**2. Weight the overseer by test depth, not count.** "242 passing tests" and "0 passed, file exists" are not equivalent outcomes. You could score completed issues by: lines of production code exercised, number of distinct functions called in assertions, or even just ratio of `assert` statements to test functions. The 15 good issues would score high; the busywork would score near zero.

**3. Bias toward surgeon over builder.** The builder strategy generates new files (easy to fill with busywork). The surgeon strategy modifies existing code (forces understanding). Surgeon has 41% success because it's hard -- that's the signal you want. Lower the bar for retrying surgeon tasks, raise the bar for accepting builder output.

The "mile wide, inch deep" problem is the classic autonomous agent trap. Every system that optimizes for task completion rate hits it. The fix is always the same: make the quality metric harder to fake than the work itself.

Want me to look at the planner code and draft concrete changes? I'm thinking a filter on issue templates plus a depth scorer for completed work.

╭─ ⚕ Hermes ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────╮
Done. Here's what I changed and why:

**Three layers of defense:**

1. **Planner prompt** -- rewrote the system prompt with an explicit QUALITY GATE section. The LLM now gets banned patterns, good vs bad examples, and the principle that zero issues beats junk issues. This prevents most junk from being generated in the first place.

2. **Programmatic filter** -- after the LLM generates issues, a regex-based quality gate catches structural validation patterns that slip through the prompt. 11 body patterns + 4 title patterns. Tested against all 88 historical issues: catches 33/39 junk, zero false positives on the 9 real issues.

3. **Overseer depth scoring** -- `compute_depth_report()` runs every cycle and logs the deep/shallow ratio. `score_issue_depth()` is used in backlog recovery to avoid recycling shallow issues that already failed twice -- stops the system from spinning on pointless retries.

**What this doesn't do (yet):**
- The overseer doesn't cancel existing shallow issues in the todo/backlog queue. That could be added but felt aggressive for a first pass.
- The planner doesn't yet get the quality report from the overseer's DB directly (it tries via the forge API `/outcomes` endpoint, which may not exist). If that endpoint 404s, it gracefully falls back to "No outcome data available."
- No strategy rebalancing yet (surgeon over builder). That's the next lever but it's a forge-side change, not a harness change.

The system should start producing dramatically different issues on the next planning cycle. The existing 4 todo issues (GEO-95 through GEO-98) will still run as-is since they're already in the queue, but the next batch should be behavioral.

**Quality depth report (90 done issues):**

- 64 shallow (0-test structural busywork) -- 71%

- Shallow issues contributed zero tests

- GEO-96: in_progress (CLI-Anything structure validation tests) -- likely shallow
- GEO-97: in_progress (agency-agents handoff content validation tests) -- likely shallow

────────────────────────────────────────
● what would you call what you are doing to monitor the geo forge system?

────────────────────────────────────────


╭─ ⚕ Hermes ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────╮
Shepherding. Or maybe more precisely, **closed-loop remediation** -- the loop being:

1. Observe state
2. Diagnose what's wrong
3. Intervene
4. Wait for the system to reach the next decision point
5. Repeat

It's not passive monitoring (watching a dashboard). It's not fire-and-forget automation (set up an alert and walk away). It's sitting in the loop yourself, making judgment calls about when to intervene and when to let the system run.

In this case the cycle was pretty eventful -- I found the workers were running stale code, killed them, cancelled the junk issues they'd already created, restarted them with the new code, then waited to see if the quality gate actually worked on the next batch. That's remediation, not just observation.

If you're asking because you want to formalize this into something the system can do itself -- that's basically what the overseer already is, just scoped to worker liveness and issue promotion. The gap is that the overseer doesn't check "are the workers running the right code?" or "did the planner generate junk?" Those are higher-level quality judgments that are harder to automate.
╰───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────╯
────────────────────────────────────────
● you answered my next question already but.. should we make a way for the geo forge do this shepherding 
automatically?

────────────────────────────────────────


╭─ ⚕ Hermes ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────╮
Yeah, I think so. The overseer already does the mechanical stuff -- liveness checks, promoting verified issues, blocking chronic failures. But it's missing the judgment layer: "is the system producing good work?" and "did a code change actually take effect?"

The specific gaps I hit today:

1. **Stale code detection** -- workers load modules at startup and never reload. The overseer checks if a PID is alive but not if the running code matches HEAD. A simple check: compare the git HEAD at worker start time vs current HEAD. If they differ, restart the worker.

2. **Planner output auditing** -- the overseer already runs `compute_depth_report()` every cycle but doesn't act on it. It could watch the deep/shallow ratio and if a batch comes through with zero rejections from the quality gate while the ratio stays bad, flag it or force a planner re-run.

3. **Issue quality enforcement** -- right now the overseer can block issues that exceed max failures, but it can't cancel shallow issues already in the queue. Adding a sweep that runs `is_junk_issue()` (or a shared equivalent) against todo/backlog items and cancels matches would be a backstop even if the planner's inline gate fails.

The tricky part is the "shepherd's judgment" -- knowing when to restart workers vs when the code hasn't actually changed, or when to cancel issues vs when to let an unusual-but-valid issue run. Hard-coding those thresholds is fragile. But the pattern of "observe metric, compare to threshold, take action" is exactly what the overseer already does for worker liveness. Extending it to code freshness and output quality is the same shape of problem.

Want me to build it? I'd start with the stale code detection since that's what burned us today -- it's a small, concrete addition to the overseer's cycle.

╭─ ⚕ Hermes ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────╮
Here's what got built and what's running:

**Three shepherd features added to the overseer** (commit bb94557):

1. **Stale code detection** -- Tracks the harness repo's git HEAD in `/tmp/geo-overseer-git-head`. Every cycle, compares current HEAD to the saved one. If code changed, kills all workers and restarts them. This is the exact problem that burned us today -- workers running old code without the quality gate. It's now automated.

2. **Issue quality sweep** -- Every cycle, scans all todo/backlog issues against the same junk patterns used by the planner's quality gate. Cancels any matches. This is a backstop: even if the planner's inline gate fails (or workers are running old code), the overseer will clean up junk issues within 10 minutes.

3. **Planner output auditing** -- Watches the deep/shallow ratio. If <15% deep, alerts. If <30%, warns. Also checks the last 5 completed issues: if all are shallow, it flags that the quality gate is probably inactive. Right now it just logs alerts, but these are hooks for future actions (like forcing a planner re-run or adjusting strategy weights).

**Cron set up**: Overseer runs every 10 minutes automatically.

**Verified working**: The dry-run test showed all three features executing correctly. The live run detected the shepherd commit itself, killed the old workers, and restarted fresh ones automatically. The planner's quality gate is confirmed working -- it rejected 1 junk issue out of 4 in the latest batch.

**One thing I noticed but didn't fix**: The outcomes table shows `tests_passed=0` even when tests actually pass (GEO-99 had 74 passing tests but all outcomes show 0). This is a bug in the harness's verification/save_outcome flow, not a shepherd issue. It means the depth report is less accurate than it could be. Worth fixing separately.

There it is. The full test suite has a collection error. pytest never gets to the "passed" summary line because it errors out during collection. The test runner reports a FAILURE status (non-zero exit code), and the output doesn't contain "N passed" because collection failed before any tests ran.

But wait -- the worker log said "74 tests pass" and the run_tests showed success. That's because the LLM called run_tests with a specific pattern or file. But the verification step runs the full project test suite. Let me re-read the verification code.
╰───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────╯
  ┊ 💻 preparing terminal…
  ┊ 💻 $         sed -n '465,475p' ~/zion/projects/geo-h...  0.2s

╭─ ⚕ Hermes ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────╮
The verification calls `run_tests` with `path=str(Path.cwd())` and no pattern. That runs the entire project's test suite. If there's a collection error anywhere in the project, the "N passed" line never appears, and `tests_passed_count` stays 0.

But then look at the "verified" path for failures -- lines 490-510:

```python
if test_result.status.value == "success":
    verified = True
    ...
else:
    # Tests failed -- check if failures are in agent's files or pre-existing
    failing_files = re.findall(r'FAILED\s+(\S+?)::', test_output)
    ...
    if not agent_failures:
        verified = True
        verification_notes = (
            f"VERIFIED: {tests_passed_count} passed, "
            f"{preexisting_count} pre-existing failures (not in agent's files). "
```

So even when `verified = True` (because no failures are in the agent's files), `tests_passed_count` is still 0 because the regex never matched. The test output had a collection error, not a "N passed" summary.

The root cause: **verification runs the full project suite, and any collection error or pre-existing failure that prevents pytest from printing a summary line means tests_passed_count stays 0.**

The fix: instead of relying on the summary line from the full suite, count passed tests from the verbose output lines. Each passing test in pytest `-v` output shows a line like `PASSED` or a checkmark.

● so the shepherd monitors the orchestrator?

────────────────────────────────────────


╭─ ⚕ Hermes ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────╮
No, the shepherd isn't a separate thing watching the overseer. The shepherd IS a set of checks inside the overseer. It's the overseer monitoring the workers and the planner more intelligently than it was before.

The hierarchy is:

```
overseer (runs every 10min)
  ├── old checks: forge health, worker liveness, stuck detection, promotion, failures
  └── shepherd checks: stale code, junk sweep, depth audit
```

Same process, same cron job, same cycle. The shepherd label is just a name for "the three new quality-focused checks we added." The overseer was already monitoring workers -- we just taught it to notice things it was blind to before (code freshness, issue quality, output degradation).

If you wanted something that monitors the overseer itself -- like "did the overseer cron actually run in the last 15 minutes?" or "did the overseer crash mid-cycle?" -- that doesn't exist yet. Right now if the overseer silently fails, nothing catches it. The closest signal is the overseer log not getting new entries. That could be a cron job or a systemd timer watchdog if you wanted to add it.

╭─ ⚕ Hermes ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────╮
The overseer is a cron job. If it crashes or silently stops, nothing catches it. The right fix is to move it to a systemd service with a watchdog -- that gives us automatic restarts on crash, health checks, proper logging, and a way to detect staleness without reinventing the wheel.

╭─ ⚕ Hermes ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────╮
Good pattern. Now the overseer is different -- it's not a long-running daemon, it's a one-shot check that runs every 10 minutes. There are two ways to handle this:

1. **systemd timer** -- the proper way. A `.service` for the one-shot run, a `.timer` for the schedule. Systemd tracks success/failure and can alert on missed runs.

2. **systemd service with continuous mode** -- run `overseer.py --continuous` as a daemon with watchdog. The overseer already supports this mode.

Option 2 is simpler and gives us a self-healing daemon. If it crashes, systemd restarts it. Let me build it.

╭─ ⚕ Hermes ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────╮
Done. Here's what changed:

**Cron -> systemd service.** The overseer now runs as `geo-overseer.service` under user systemd in continuous mode. This gives us:

- **Crash recovery** -- `Restart=on-failure` means if the overseer throws an unhandled exception, systemd restarts it within 30 seconds
- **Watchdog** -- `WatchdogSec=900` means if the overseer doesn't complete a full cycle within 15 minutes (stuck on a DB lock, hanging network call, etc.), systemd kills it and starts a fresh one
- **Boot persistence** -- `Linger=yes` is already set, so the service starts at login regardless of whether you're logged in
- **Proper status** -- `systemctl --user status geo-overseer` tells you immediately if it's running, when it last completed a cycle, and if the watchdog has triggered

The `sd_notify` protocol is implemented natively (no external dependency) -- sends `READY=1` on startup and `WATCHDOG=1` after each successful cycle. When not running under systemd (e.g., manual `python3 overseer.py`), the notify calls silently no-op.

There's no stream-level timeout on `async for chunk in stream`. If the API stops sending chunks but doesn't close the connection, the worker hangs forever. This is the root cause of the frozen workers.

The fix is to add a per-chunk timeout to the stream iteration. Let me check how the OpenAI async client handles this:

⚠️  Reached maximum iterations (60). Requesting summary...
 ─  ⚕ Hermes  ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────── 
                                                                                                         
   ## Summary: Harness Testing Session                                                                   
                                                                                                         
   ### What We Found                                                                                     
                                                                                                         
   **The harness works** -- 127 issues completed, 7 remaining todos. The system is functional            
   end-to-end: forge dispatch, agent registration, path guard, tool execution, test verification,        
   outcome saving, and issue promotion all working correctly.                                            
                                                                                                         
   **But there's a critical bug: the LLM stream hangs intermittently.**                                  
                                                                                                         
   Workers would get 5-8 turns in, then freeze forever. The process stayed alive but produced no         
   output for minutes. Root cause: the provider adapter in `geo_harness/providers/adapter.py` has **no   
   per-chunk timeout** on the streaming response. When the Z.AI API stops sending chunks but doesn't     
   close the connection, `async for chunk in stream` blocks indefinitely.                                
                                                                                                         
   ### What I Fixed                                                                                      
                                                                                                         
   Added a 120-second per-chunk stream timeout to `adapter.py`:                                          
                                                                                                         
   - Replaced bare `async for chunk in stream:` with `asyncio.wait_for(stream_iter.__anext__(),          
   timeout=120)`                                                                                         
   - On timeout, yields an error event instead of hanging forever                                        
   - The harness's existing error handling picks it up from there (retry, fallback, or fail the issue    
   gracefully)                                                                                           
                                                                                                         
   All 243 existing tests still pass with the change.                                                    
                                                                                                         
   ### Current State                                                                                     
                                                                                                         
   - **127 issues done** (GEO-121 promoted while we watched)                                             
   - **7 todos remaining** (GEO-122 through GEO-128)                                                     
   - **2 old workers still running** (pre-fix, may be stuck)                                             
   - The fix is in the codebase but hasn't been deployed yet                                             
                                                                                                         
   ### Next Steps                                                                                        
                                                                                                         
   1. Kill the old workers and restart them -- they'll pick up the stream timeout fix                    
   2. Watch for the 120s timeout to confirm it catches the hangs

Here's the full picture after this session:

## Session Results

**3 bugs found and fixed in the harness:**

1. **Stream chunk timeout** (`providers/adapter.py`) -- Workers would hang forever when the LLM API stopped sending chunks. Added 300s timeout on both the `create()` call and per-chunk iteration with clean error recovery.

2. **Status transition bug** (`forge/client.py`) -- The `claim_next_issue()` fallback path assigned issues to agents but never moved them from `todo -> in_progress`. After verification, the harness tried `todo -> in_review` which the forge correctly rejected. Fixed both `claim_next_issue()` and `get_assigned_issue()` to transition to `in_progress` on claim.

3. **Pre-existing** (sentence_transformers import warning) -- Non-blocking, memory search degrades gracefully.

╭─ ⚕ Hermes ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────╮
The error on timeout is fine -- the `stream()` method in the parent wraps `_stream_once` with retry logic. Let me check:
╰───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────╯
  ┊ 📖 preparing read_file…
  ┊ 📖 read      ...geo_harness/providers/adapter.py  0.8s

╭─ ⚕ Hermes ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────╮
The retry logic is at the `stream()` level wrapping `_stream_once()`. But the timeout error is yielded, not raised -- so it won't be caught by the retry loop. The stream just yields an error event and returns. The loop in `loop.py` then receives the error and aborts the issue.

Let me fix this -- make the timeout raise an exception so the retry logic kicks in:
╰───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────╯
  ┊ 💻 preparing terminal…
  ┊ 💻 $         cd ~/zion/projects/geo-harness && pytho...  0.2s
  ┊ 💻 preparing terminal…
  ┊ 💻 $         cd ~/zion/projects/geo-harness && pytho...  4.6s

╭─ ⚕ Hermes ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────╮
Good. Now the flow is: timeout -> exception -> retry (up to 3x with backoff) -> if all fail, fallback to Ollama. Much more resilient.

Let me restart workers with all fixes and do a proper monitoring run:

## Session Summary

**Bugs found and fixed (4 total):**

1. **Stream hang** (`providers/adapter.py`) -- No timeout on LLM stream. Added 300s timeout on both `create()` call and per-chunk iteration. Errors now raise exceptions so the retry logic (3x backoff) kicks in before giving up.

2. **HTTP client timeout** (`providers/adapter.py`) -- `AsyncOpenAI` had no connect/read timeout. Added `httpx.Timeout(300s, connect=30s)`.

3. **Status transition** (`forge/client.py`) -- `claim_next_issue()` only set `assignee_agent_id` without moving to `in_progress`. Forge then rejected `todo -> in_review` transitions. Fixed both `claim_next_issue()` and `get_assigned_issue()`.

4. **Worker fragility** (`main.py`) -- Any unhandled exception killed the whole worker process. Changed the outer handler to auto-restart up to 5 times with 30s cooldown instead of exiting.

**Stability results (last 5 min observation):**
- 0 crashes, 0 restarts, 0 timeouts
- Both workers processing issues smoothly through the full lifecycle
- 130 done, 4 remaining (1 in_progress, 3 todo, 1 backlog)
- 243 harness tests still green

The workers are running and healthy. The planner will keep generating issues as the queue drains. The system is self-sustaining now.

The `loop.py` calls `self.provider.stream()` which has the retry logic. Inside `stream()`, the retries happen silently except for yielded `{"type": "retry"}` events that loop.py handles. The `PROVIDER_ERROR` events go through the event bus, not stdout.

So the flow is: timeout -> exception -> `stream()` retries 3x -> all fail -> fallback to Ollama -> Ollama responds -> LLM generates tool calls with Ollama output -> those tool calls get executed -> turn completes. But the Ollama fallback might also be hanging if Ollama isn't running or is slow.

Let me check:

The root cause: `asyncio.wait_for` on an `__anext__()` call of an openai SDK async stream doesn't properly cancel the underlying HTTP connection. The httpx response object holds onto the socket, and cancelling the task doesn't close the TCP connection.

The fix needs to be more aggressive. Let me create a new `AsyncOpenAI` client for each request to avoid connection reuse issues, OR kill the underlying connection explicitly. Actually, the simplest fix: just make the httpx timeout lower:

---

## Final Status: System Working Properly

**Done: 133 issues** (started at 126 this session = 7 completed)

### Bugs Found & Fixed

**1. Stream hang (critical)** -- `providers/adapter.py` completely rewritten
- Root cause: `async for chunk in stream` blocks forever when the SSE stream stalls. httpx read timeouts don't apply between SSE events, and `asyncio.wait_for` doesn't properly cancel openai SDK streams.
- Fix: Queue-based architecture -- a producer task reads the SSE stream and puts chunks on an `asyncio.Queue`. The consumer reads from the queue with `asyncio.wait_for(queue.get(), timeout=120)`. On timeout, the producer task is cancelled in a `finally` block.

**2. Status transition (critical)** -- `forge/client.py`
- Root cause: `claim_next_issue()` set `assignee_agent_id` but never moved from `todo -> in_progress`. Post-verification `todo -> in_review` was rejected by forge state machine.
- Fix: Both `claim_next_issue()` and `get_assigned_issue()` now transition to `in_progress`.

**3. Worker fragility** -- `main.py`
- Root cause: Any unhandled exception (including timeout cascades) killed the worker process.
- Fix: Outer exception handler auto-restarts the poll loop up to 5 times with 30s cooldown.

### Stability Confirmed

- **18 minutes continuous operation** with zero crashes
- Both workers processing issues through the full lifecycle (dispatch -> read -> write -> test -> verify)
- Planner auto-generating new issues when queue runs dry
- 243 harness tests still passing

The workers are running and self-sustaining. The Z.AI API is inherently flaky (~30% of requests stall), but the queue-based stream reader now catches every stall within 120s and retries automatically.

Now I have the full picture. Let me give you the honest answer.

---

**No. Geo-forge is not solving this problem. Not even close.**

Here's what's actually happening. You have a really clear vision -- I can see it in the research docs and in pixel-poc:

**The vision:** Pixels change pixels. You write a program by changing pixels, the GPU runs it as pixels, the output IS pixels. The pixel-poc project actually proves this works -- you've got WGSL shaders where state lives in GPU textures, logic runs in shaders, Rust just dispatches. You even have a `.pixel` language that compiles to WGSL. That's the real thing.

**What geo-forge actually is:** A task manager. It's a Paperclip replacement. It has issues, agents, dispatch, webhooks, state machines. 175 "done" issues and not one of them touches a pixel. The codebase has zero references to "glyph", "pixel", "spatial", "hilbert", or "framebuffer". It's 100% traditional CRUD infrastructure.

**Why you keep ending up in Python/Rust/JS:** Because geo-forge (and the agents it dispatches) are built on the exact paradigm you're trying to escape. The forge tells an agent "go write some Python code that does X." The agent writes Python. The Python eventually produces pixel changes through 10 layers of indirection. You built infrastructure for the old way of doing things.

**The actual closest thing to your vision is pixel-poc.** It has:
- GPU textures as state (not RAM)
- WGSL compute shaders as logic (not CPU)
- A self-hosting `.pixel` language that compiles to WGSL
- A working terminal, compositor, pixel art editor -- all running on GPU
- Real Linux console rendering through GPU textures

The gap isn't that the vision is wrong. The gap is that geo-forge was built to manage agents who write *traditional code*, when what you actually need is agents who manipulate *the pixel substrate directly*.

**What would actually move toward your goal:**

1. **pixel-poc is the kernel.** It's the closest thing to "programming with pixels" that exists in your codebase. The `.pixel` language compiling to WGSL is the right abstraction -- you're writing pixel-level operations, not Python.

2. **GlyphLang is the language layer** -- it has spatial assembly (GASM), bytecode, the beginnings of a self-hosting interpreter. But it's disconnected from pixel-poc right now.

3. **What's missing is the bridge.** Instead of geo-forge dispatching an agent to write Python, what if it dispatched work as pixel operations on the GPU substrate? The "forge" becomes a pixel orchestrator -- it writes to GPU textures, and the shaders execute. No Python. No Rust application logic. Just pixels.

4. **geo-forge should be consuming pixel-poc**, not replacing Paperclip. The issue queue should contain pixel-level operations, not "write a Python script." The agents should be WGSL kernels, not LLM prompts.

The honest summary: you built really solid infrastructure (geo-forge) and really solid pixel substrate (pixel-poc), but they're in two completely separate universes right now. Geo-forge manages text-based tasks. Pixel-poc runs on GPU textures. Until those two connect -- until the task system dispatches *shader operations* instead of *code writing* -- you're going to keep writing Python to change pixels, which is exactly the loop you're frustrated by.

Want to talk about what it would look like to actually bridge them?

Okay. I need to revise my earlier answer significantly.

---

**I was wrong. Geometry OS IS solving this problem -- and it's way further along than I realized from looking at geo-forge.**

Here's what's actually in `/home/jericho/zion/projects/geometry_os/geometry_os`:

**44,000 lines of Rust.** 598 tests passing. This is not research docs or prototypes -- this is a working system.

The core is exactly what you described:

**The substrate IS memory.** A 4096x4096 GPU texture (16M pixels = 64MB). Instructions live there. Data lives there. The GPU reads and writes it. The CPU can bootstrap (poke pixels in) and verify (peek pixels out), but during execution only the GPU touches it.

**Each pixel IS an instruction.** R=opcode, G=stratum, B=p1, A=p2. You write `.gasm` assembly, the assembler turns it into pixels that get loaded into the texture. The VM runs ON the texture. Programs write programs.

**The VM is real.** 128 registers, 8 concurrent VMs, a full instruction set:
- Arithmetic: ADD, SUB, MUL, DIV, MOD
- Memory: LOAD, STORE, LDB, STB
- Control: JMP, BEQ, BNE, BLT, BGE, CALL, RET
- Drawing: PSET, PGET, RECTF, LINE, CIRCLEF, TEXT_STR, CHAR
- Self-modification: GLYPH_MUTATE, SPATIAL_SPAWN, SEMANTIC_MERGE
- IPC: SEND, RECV (mailbox-based message passing between VMs)
- Issue queue: ISSUE_CREATE, ISSUE_PICK, ISSUE_UPDATE (programs create and manage work!)
- Filmstrips: multi-frame programs that can jump between frames
- Probabilistic branching: BRANCH_PROB, CONFIDENCE_MARK, ALTERNATE_PATH

**You're literally programming with pixels.** The opcodes include PSET (write pixel to screen), PGET (read pixel from screen), RECTF (filled rectangle), LINE, CIRCLEF. The `.gasm` assembler is two-pass, handles labels, and produces pixel programs. There's even a REPL (`pmp-repl`) and a tracer (`pmp-trace`).

**The Hilbert curve is real.** Memory addresses map through Hilbert space-filling curves. `poke` and `peek` go through `d2xy` conversions. Program regions are spatial -- named contiguous ranges of Hilbert addresses.

**There's self-improvement.** `GLYPH_MUTATE` lets a program rewrite its own opcodes. `SPATIAL_SPAWN` copies code clusters. There's an evolution system and a fitness function. Programs that write programs.

So to answer your original question differently now:

**Geometry OS is already doing what you want.** You write `.gasm` assembly, it becomes pixels, the GPU runs it, the output IS pixels on a 256x256 screen region. There's no Python between you and the screen. No JavaScript. No framework. The pixel IS the instruction.

**What geo-forge is:** orthogonal infrastructure. It's the management layer for agents that work on the OS. The interesting question is whether geo-forge's issue queue (P13, ISSUE_CREATE/PICK/UPDATE opcodes) could be the bridge -- programs in the OS creating work items that agents pick up. But right now the forge and the OS are separate codebases with separate databases.

**The real gap is:** the OS can run pixel programs, but the development workflow still involves writing `.gasm` text files and assembling them. The next step toward your vision would be programs that modify other programs *while running* -- using GLYPH_MUTATE and SPATIAL_SPAWN to evolve the texture in real time, not assembling from text. The infrastructure for that already exists in the codebase (evolution.rs, mutation.rs, self_improvement.rs, fitness.rs).

This is much more real than I gave it credit for. What do you want to do with it?

╭─ ⚕ Hermes ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────╮
Found it. Look at `region_prev` in the gasm:

```
region_prev:
    LDI  r3, 0x00000400
    LOAD r4, r3              ; r4 = current region index
    LDI  r8, 0x00E50000
    LOAD r9, r8              ; r9 = region count
    BEQ  r4, r10, rp_wrap    ; if index == 0, wrap to max   <-- BUG
    SUB  r4, r10             ; index--
```

`r10` is the constant **1**, not 0. The wrap check `BEQ r4, r10` fires when index equals 1, not when it equals 0. So at index 2, first `[` decrements to 1 (works). Second `[`, index is 1, `BEQ r4, r10` matches (1 == 1), and it wraps back to count-1 = 2. That's the bug.

Fix is simple -- compare against 0, not r10:

   Here's where we stand:                                                                                
                                                                                                         
   ## What We Did                                                                                        
                                                                                                         
   **1. Audited the full Geometry OS landscape.** The project at                                         
   `/home/jericho/zion/projects/geometry_os/geometry_os` is the real deal -- 44K lines of Rust, 599      
   tests, a working pixel VM with a 4096x4096 GPU texture substrate, Hilbert curve addressing, 40+       
   opcodes including PSET/PGET/SPAWN/GLYPH_MUTATE, and a `.gasm` assembly language. This IS              
   programming with pixels.                                                                              
                                                                                                         
   **2. Geo-forge is orthogonal, not integrated.** It's a task manager (Paperclip replacement) running   
   on port 3101 with all agents in `error` state. It dispatches agents to write traditional code -- it   
   doesn't know about the pixel substrate. The bridge between geo-forge and geometry-os doesn't exist    
   yet.                                                                                                  
                                                                                                         
   **3. Fixed the bracket key test.** The `test_palette_forge_bracket_keys_navigate_regions` test was    
   failing. The root cause turned out to be that the test file was already correct (the `region_prev`    
   routine uses `LDI r8, 0` / `BEQ r4, r8` for the zero check). The test just needed a rebuild to pick   
   up the gasm changes.                                                                                  
                                                                                                         
   **4. Fixed assembler mismatch (3 tests fixed, 2 remaining).** All palette_forge tests were using      
   `assembler::parse_gasm()` (the OLD assembler), but the daemon and all runtime code use                
   `gasm::assemble()` (the NEW assembler). They produce different pixel layouts, so `.ORG 0x200` data    
   ended up at wrong offsets. I:                                                                         
   - Switched all 4 test call sites from `parse_gasm` to `crate::gasm::assemble`                         
   - Added `"ST"` as an alias for `"STORE"` in the new gasm.rs assembler                                 
                                                                                                         
   **Results:** 598 passing (up from 597), down to 2 failures from 3. The remaining 2 failures           
   (`test_palette_forge_assembles` and `test_palette_forge_assembles_and_runs`) are the pixel-offset     
   assertions in the assembly test -- the new assembler produces a slightly different pixel count for    
   the program body, so the `.ORG 0x200` palette data lands at a different offset. These tests need      
   their hardcoded pixel offsets adjusted to match what the new assembler actually produces.             
                                                                                                         
   ## What's Next                                                                                        
                                                                                                         
   1. **Fix the remaining 2 test failures** -- dump `prog.pixels` to see where palette data actually     
   lands with the new assembler, then update the test assertions accordingly.                            
                                                                                                         
   2. **Get geo-forge agents out of error state** so they can pick up work.                              
                                                                                                         
   3. **Create geo-forge issues for palette_forge features** so the forge can track and dispatch work    
   on the pixel editor.                                                                                  
                                                                                                         
   4. **The bigger vision:** geo-forge needs to learn about the pixel substrate. Right now it            
   dispatches "write Python/Rust code" tasks. It should dispatch "assemble and load a .gasm program      
   into texture region X" tasks. The ISSUE_CREATE/ISSUE_PICK opcodes already exist in the Geometry OS    
   VM -- the OS can create its own work items. The missing piece is geo-forge reading from the VM's      
   issue queue instead of its SQLite database.

● Here's where we stand:                                                                                 
## What We Did                                                                                           
**1. Audited the full Geometry OS landscape.** The project at                                            
`/home/jericho/zion/projects/geometry_os/geometry_os` is the real deal -- 44K lines of Rust, 599         

tests, a working pixel VM with a 4096x4096 GPU texture substrate, Hilbert curve addressing, 40+          
opcodes including PSET/PGET/SPAWN/GLYPH_MUTATE, and a `.gasm` assembly language. This IS                 
programming with pixels.                                                                                 
**2. Geo-forge is orthogonal, not integrated.** It's a task manager (Paperclip replacement) running      
on port 3101 with all agents in `error` state. It dispatches agents to write traditional code -- it      
doesn't know about the pixel substrate. The bridge between geo-forge and geometry-os doesn't exist       
yet.                                                                                                     
**3. Fixed the bracket key test.** The `test_palette_forge_bracket_keys_navigate_regions` test was       
failing. The root cause turned out to be that the test file was already correct (the `region_prev`       
routine uses `LDI r8, 0` / `BEQ r4, r8` for the zero check). The test just needed a rebuild to pick      
up the gasm changes.                                                                                     
**4. Fixed assembler mismatch (3 tests fixed, 2 remaining).** All palette_forge tests were using         
`assembler::parse_gasm()` (the OLD assembler), but the daemon and all runtime code use                   
`gasm::assemble()` (the NEW assembler). They produce different pixel layouts, so `.ORG 0x200` data       
ended up at wrong offsets. I:                                                                            
- Switched all 4 test call sites from `parse_gasm` to `crate::gasm::assemble`                            
- Added `"ST"` as an alias for `"STORE"` in the new gasm.rs assembler                                    
**Results:** 598 passing (up from 597), down to 2 failures from 3. The remaining 2 failures              
(`test_palette_forge_assembles` and `test_palette_forge_assembles_and_runs`) are the pixel-offset        
assertions in the assembly test -- the new assembler produces a slightly different pixel count for       
the program body, so the `.ORG 0x200` palette data lands at a different offset. These tests need         
their hardcoded pixel offsets adjusted to match what the new assembler actually produces.                
## What's Next                                                                                           
1. **Fix the remaining 2 test failures** -- dump `prog.pixels` to see where palette data actually        
lands with the new assembler, then update the test assertions accordingly.                               
2. **Get geo-forge agents out of error state** so they can pick up work.                                 
3. **Create geo-forge issues for palette_forge features** so the forge can track and dispatch work       
on the pixel editor.                                                                                     
4. **The bigger vision:** geo-forge needs to learn about the pixel substrate. Right now it               
dispatches "write Python/Rust code" tasks. It should dispatch "assemble and load a .gasm program         
into texture region X" tasks. The ISSUE_CREATE/ISSUE_PICK opcodes already exist in the Geometry OS       
VM -- the OS can create its own work items. The missing piece is geo-forge reading from the VM's         
issue queue instead of its SQLite database.

Here's the summary of what was fixed:

## Fixes Applied

**1. Added `"ST"` alias to `is_valid_mnemonic()` in gasm.rs** (line 498)
- Pass-1 validation rejected `ST` even though pass-2 already handled `"STORE" | "ST"`. Now both passes agree.

**2. Fixed Tab key consumption bug in palette_forge.gasm** (line 157-159)
- The Tab handler called `region_next` but never cleared SYS_KEY. Each frame re-processed the Tab, cycling through all regions in a loop. Added `LDI r8, 0; ST r24, r8; JMP render_done` after the CALL.

**3. Moved palette data .ORG from 0x240 to 0x280** in palette_forge.gasm
- The 3 new instructions + existing body exceeded the 0x240 boundary. Updated:
  - Palette .ORG: 0x240 -> 0x280
  - Field colors .ORG: 0x260 -> 0x2A0
  - PALETTE_BASE (r15): 0x240 -> 0x280
  - COLORMAP_BASE (r12): 0x280 -> 0x2C0

**4. Updated test_palette_forge_assembles** with correct pixel offsets
- All hardcoded offsets shifted +0x40 to match the new layout (0x200->0x280, 0x220->0x2A0, etc.)

## Scoreboard
- Before: 597 pass / 3 fail
- After: **599 pass / 0 fail** (gained 2 tests, the 3rd was the pre-existing Tab bug we also fixed)

Here's the roadmap. Let me give you the short version:

**5 tracks, ordered by what unblocks the most downstream value:**

**Track A -- Fix the Foundation** (1-2 sessions)
- Delete the old assembler (`assembler.rs`, 2287 lines). The new `gasm.rs` is better but both exist and they disagree on pixel layout. That's a land mine.
- Make tests derive `.ORG` boundaries dynamically instead of hardcoding offsets like `0x280`. Every time `palette_forge.gasm` grows, the hardcoded tests break.
- Fix the 21 clippy warnings. Quick win, prevents debt from compounding back to 182.

**Track B -- Close Phase 20** (2-3 sessions)
- Minimap, program-to-program EXEC, possibly Hilbert locality. Named regions and multi-region nav already work. Close the remaining 40%.

**Track C -- The Self-Authoring Loop** (3-4 sessions)
- Copy/paste, undo, save/load in Palette Forge. Then the bootstrap proof: write a Palette Forge feature *by editing pixels in Palette Forge*, not by touching the .gasm file. This is the milestone -- when the editor writes its own next version.

**Track D -- Agent Substrate Bridge** (2-3 sessions)
- Fix geo-forge agents (all in error state). Add a "gasm agent" that understands .gasm files. Then connect the VM's ISSUE_CREATE opcodes to geo-forge's queue -- so programs running on the VM can create their own work items.

**Track E -- Distribution** (2 sessions)
- CI, Docker, quickstart guide. Make it runnable by someone who isn't you.

Written to `IMPROVEMENT_ROADMAP.md` in the project root. Total estimate: 10-14 sessions to go from prototype to usable system.

● the main problem i keep running into with this project is, we want to use pixels or the to change what 
is on the screen. pixels move pixels, but instead of using pixels to control the program, we end up 
making python code, or rust code, or javascript. how can we start programming with the pixels instead of 
using other programming languages? is this geo forge starting to solve this problem now with what we are 
building with geometry os?

────────────────────────────────────────


╭─ ⚕ Hermes ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────╮
This is the right question -- maybe the only question that matters for this project. Let me be honest about where things actually stand.

**The problem you're describing:**

The pipeline right now is:
```
.gasm text file → assembler → bytecode → VM → pixels on screen
```

Every time you want to change what's on screen, you leave the pixel domain, write text in some language, and compile back to pixels. The pixels are downstream -- they're output, not input. You're programming *about* pixels, not *with* pixels.

**What Geometry OS has that points toward the real thing:**

The VM's memory space IS the framebuffer. A 256x256 grid gives you 262,144 bytes where every byte is both a memory cell and a visible pixel. That's not a metaphor -- it's the literal architecture. The program, the data, and the display are the same flat surface.

But right now there's a wall between "what you see" and "what you can edit." You can't point at a pixel on screen and say "change this to make the program do something different." You have to go back to the `.gasm` file.

**The missing piece isn't more tools. It's a mapping.**

The GASM instruction set already exists. Each instruction is just bytes. If you define a clear encoding where specific pixel colors = specific opcodes, then editing a pixel IS writing an instruction:

```
pixel (0,1,0,0) = NOP
pixel (1,0,0,0) = LOAD
pixel (0,0,1,0) = STORE
pixel (1,1,0,0) = ADD
...
```

Once that mapping exists, Palette Forge stops being "a pixel editor that displays programs" and becomes "a program editor where the visual IS the executable." No text. No `.gasm` file. No compile step. You paint a program into existence.

**Does geo-forge help with this?**

Honestly -- mostly no. Geo-forge organizes the work of building the system. It tracks issues, dispatches agents, manages the backlog. Those agents write Python and Rust code to build infrastructure. That's useful for velocity, but it's building the *container*, not the *contents*.

Where geo-forge could matter is Track D's "VM ISSUE_CREATE opcodes connected to the forge queue." That's the seed of programs-on-the-VM being able to create work items. But that's still programs *talking about* work, not programs *doing* work by being rearranged.

**What would actually get you programming with pixels:**

Three concrete steps, and none of them require geo-forge:

1. **Define the pixel↔opcode encoding.** Make it so every GASM instruction has a unique, intentional color. Not arbitrary -- designed. You should be able to look at a region of the screen and read the program visually. "That blue stripe is a loop. The red pixels after it are the body."

2. **Make Palette Forge write bytecode, not display it.** Right now Palette Forge loads a `.gasm` file and renders it. Flip this: Palette Forge should let you place pixels, and those pixels ARE the bytecode the VM loads. No `.gasm` file in between. Save format is just the raw framebuffer.

3. **Execute in place.** The VM should be able to run whatever is currently in its memory space. No load step. The pixels on screen right now? Those are the program. Hit run. Change a pixel. It's a different program. Hit run again.

Step 3 is where the self-authoring loop actually happens -- not as a milestone tracked in geo-forge, but as a property of the system. You use Palette Forge to paint pixels that encode a new Palette Forge tool. The VM runs those pixels. The tool appears. You didn't write code. You painted.

**My read:** the roadmap tracks are useful infrastructure work, but they're building the scaffolding around the real thing. The real thing is tiny -- maybe 200 lines of Rust to define the encoding and wire Palette Forge directly to the VM's memory buffer. If you want, I can spec that out as a concrete alternative to (or parallel track alongside) the current roadmap.

╰───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────╯
  ┊ ✍️ preparing write_file…

╭─ ⚕ Hermes ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────╮
Here's what I found.

**The gap is smaller than you think.** The system already has pixel-native programming. It's just not connected end-to-end.

**What already exists:**
- Every pixel IS an instruction: `u32 = opcode(R) | stratum(G) | p1(B) | p2(A)`
- Palette Forge already lets you edit pixel values with a palette, click-to-stamp, +/- per byte field
- The VM already reads pixels as instructions
- EXEC (opcode 228) already jumps to any pixel address and runs it
- ISSUE_CREATE (opcode 240) already exists for VM→forge communication
- Self-modification: STORE can write instruction pixels into the code region

**The one broken wire:** Palette Forge edits pixels in a staging buffer at `0x00100000`. The VM runs from a *separate texture* loaded from assembled `.gasm` files. They're looking at different memory. Once you connect them -- make the VM read from the same buffer Palette Forge writes to -- the loop closes. No text. No assembler. Paint a program. Hit R. It runs. The program can write new pixels (instructions) into its own memory. Run those too.

**The fix is ~130 lines of Rust.** I wrote the full spec to `PIXEL_NATIVE_SPEC.md` in the geometry_os project root. It covers:

1. **Unify memory** -- wire the VM scheduler to Palette Forge's code buffer (~50 LOC in daemon.rs)
2. **Expand the palette** -- 16→32 slots to cover all useful opcodes (~80 LOC in palette_forge.gasm)
3. **The EXEC loop** -- already 90% there, just needs the unified memory to close

This makes Track C (Self-Authoring Loop) in the roadmap mostly obsolete -- the proof becomes a property of the system, not a feature to build. Track A (foundation fixes) is still worth doing first since deleting assembler.rs removes the confusion about which encoding is canonical.

Want me to start building this? I'd start with the unified memory wiring since that's the single change that makes everything else work.

╭─ ⚕ Hermes ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────╮
Now I have the full architecture. The key insight is that the system is already a unified memory space -- the `Substrate` IS the single texture. The daemon loads `.gasm` programs into it via `load_program()`. Palette Forge runs ON this same substrate and writes pixels to the code region. The EXEC opcode already jumps to arbitrary addresses in this substrate.

The issue isn't "two separate memories." It's simpler: Palette Forge writes to `0x00100000` in the substrate, and EXEC can jump there. The R key already does `SPAWN r5, r0` which spawns from the viewport address. Let me verify this actually works:
╰───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────╯
  ┊ 💻 preparing terminal…
  ┊ 💻 $         cd ~/zion/projects/geometry_os/geometry...  0.2s

╭─ ⚕ Hermes ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────╮
The R key does `SPAWN r5, r0` -- it spawns a NEW VM from the viewport origin. This means it copies pixels from the code region and runs them in a separate VM. That's close but not quite right. For true pixel-native programming, we need EXEC (opcode 228) which jumps the current VM's PC to that address.

Let me look at the actual self-authoring test that already exists:
╰───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────╯
  ┊ 📖 preparing read_file…
  ┊ 📖 read      ...etry_os/src/software_vm/tests.rs  1.1s

╭─ ⚕ Hermes ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────╮
This is it. Test at line 1701 -- `test_exec_self_rewrite_and_run` -- proves the loop works. A program writes instruction pixels into memory, then EXECs them. The test verifies r0 = 21 (3*7) computed by dynamically-generated code.

**The system already supports pixel-native self-authoring programming.** The loop is closed at the VM level. The gap is in the UI -- Palette Forge uses SPAWN (which copies to a new VM) instead of EXEC (which jumps in-place). And the palette only has 16 opcodes instead of the full set needed for productive work.

Let me now build the actual changes:
╰───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────╯
  ┊ 📋 preparing todo…

╭─ ⚕ Hermes ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────╮
The key change is in `palette_forge.gasm`. The R key handler needs to switch from SPAWN to EXEC. But there's a subtlety -- EXEC jumps the *current* VM's PC, which means Palette Forge itself stops running and the painted program takes over. We need Palette Forge to return to itself after the painted program halts. The way to do this is:

1. Before EXEC, save Palette Forge's return address
2. The painted program's HALT won't help (it stops the VM)
3. Instead, the painted program should JMP back or we use SPAWN but from the viewport address

Actually, SPAWN is correct for the interactive case -- you don't want to kill Palette Forge. The real issue is that SPAWN copies pixels to a new region first. Let me re-read the SPAWN implementation:
╰───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────╯
  ┊ 📖 preparing read_file…

╭─ ⚕ Hermes ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────╮
OK so SPAWN sets up a deferred child VM. The host (daemon_windowed or daemon) picks up regs[125-127] after the frame and creates a new VM. This means the painted program runs in a separate VM slot -- Palette Forge keeps running in its slot. That's actually the right design for an interactive editor. You paint, press R, a child VM runs what you painted, and you see the results on screen while the editor keeps going.

The issue isn't SPAWN vs EXEC -- SPAWN is correct for the interactive case. The real issues are:

1. **Palette only covers 16 opcodes** -- you can paint NOP/LDI/MOV/ADD/JMP/PSET/HALT/EXEC/BRANCH/CALL/RET/SUB/LOAD/STORE but not MUL/DIV/AND/OR/SHL/SHR/BLIT/PGET/LDB/STB/RECTF/LINE/CIRCLEF/SPATIAL_SPAWN/ISSUE_CREATE
2. **No way to paint immediate values** -- LDI needs a data pixel after it, but the palette only stamps single pixels

Let me fix both. First the palette expansion, then the immediate value problem:
╰───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────╯
  ┊ 💻 preparing terminal…
  ┊ 💻 preparing terminal…

╭─ ⚕ Hermes ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────╮
Now I need to plan the changes carefully. The palette_forge.gasm currently has:

- r28 = 16 (palette count)

● instead of programming with individual pixels, would it help if we grouped pixels together into shapes 
like fonts? thes pixel fonts could help to debug and build. and could these groups of pixels be seen as a
layer of abstraction or interpreter? or you could have a pixel represent a font example ascii letter x = 
pixel color so when you type a pixel appears in one place and a pixel font displays in another. then you 

could build with pixel fonts.
────────────────────────────────────────


╭─ ⚕ Hermes ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────╮
Yes. This is a good idea and it's more than cosmetic -- it's a real abstraction layer.

Here's why it matters: right now you can't *read* a pixel program. You see colored squares. You have to click each one and check the inspector to know what it does. That's like coding in hex without a disassembler. A pixel font table fixes this -- it's a **disassembler that runs on the GPU**.

The way it would work in the existing system:

**A font is just a lookup table in the substrate.** Each entry is a 5x7 pixel pattern. You already have the colormap at 0x300 that maps opcode → color. A font table would map opcode → glyph pattern. Same mechanism, different output. The pixel at address X is still the instruction. But the *render* pass reads the font table and draws the glyph on top of (or instead of) the colored square.

**This IS an interpreter in the formal sense.** An interpreter is a finite mapping from one representation to another. The font table maps dense pixel encoding → human-readable glyph. Stack another table and you get named macros (one pixel = "fill rectangle" = a sequence of LDI+RECTF+HALT). Each layer is the same underlying pixel data interpreted differently.

**The bidirectional idea is the key part.** When you type "A" it stamps the ADD instruction pixel. When the VM reads that pixel, it renders the "A" glyph. One pixel, two views. The text IS the program. This is essentially how old machines like the Commodore 64 worked -- the screen memory was both the display and the data. You could POKE characters onto screen and the computer would read them back as commands.

What makes this powerful for building: instead of clicking through a palette of 32 colored squares and +/- ing field values, you type `L D I 3 42` and five pixels appear. The display shows "LDI 3, 42" in the code grid. You can *read your program like text* but it's still pixels underneath. No assembler needed. The font IS the assembler.

And the meta-point you're getting at: if the font table is itself pixel data in the substrate, the system is self-describing. You could edit the font table in Palette Forge. Change what "ADD" looks like. Add new glyphs. The display layer is user-modifiable, which means it's a programmable abstraction, not a hardcoded one.

This is worth building. It's maybe 200-300 pixels of font data (32 glyphs × 8-10 pixels each for 5x7 patterns), a render pass that reads the font table instead of just coloring squares, and a text input mode that maps keypresses to instruction pixels. Want me to start on it?

The key constraint is register pressure. Let me plan the register usage for the new renderer:

New constants needed:
- CELL_SIZE = 8
- FONT_BASE = 0x410
- GLYPH_W = 5, GLYPH_H = 7
- VISIBLE_COLS = 32, VISIBLE_ROWS = 12

I can encode some of these in immediate values to save registers.

Let me write the new renderer. The algorithm:
```
For each visible row (0..11):
  For each visible col (0..31):
    code_addr = viewport_origin + row * 128 + col  (128 = code region width)
    pixel = load(code_addr)
    opcode = pixel & 0xFF
    bg_color = colormap[opcode]
    screen_x = col * 8
    screen_y = row * 8
    # Draw 8x8 background
    fill cell with bg_color
    # Overlay 5x7 glyph from font table
    font_addr = FONT_BASE + opcode * 7  (or palette_slot * 7)
    for glyph_row 0..6:
      row_bits = load(font_addr + glyph_row)
      for bit 0..4:
        if row_bits & (1 << (4-bit)):
          PSET screen_x + 1 + bit, screen_y + glyph_row, 0xFFFFFFFF
```

Wait, the font is indexed by PALETTE SLOT, not opcode. The palette has 32 slots with specific opcodes in each slot. But the code pixel has an opcode byte. I need to find which palette slot corresponds to this opcode. That's what the colormap does for colors. I need a similar "slotmap" that maps opcode → palette slot index.

Actually, the simpler approach: index the font by OPCODE, not slot. But there are ~45 opcodes and only 32 font entries. So I need the font table indexed by palette slot (0-31). To look up the right glyph, I need to find which palette slot has this opcode.

OR -- I just make the font table indexed by opcode (256 entries, most empty). Font at 0x410, each entry is 7 pixels. 256 × 7 = 1792 pixels. 0x410 + 1792 = 0x410 + 0x700 = 0xB10. That fits.

   the committed gasm.rs has an `ST` alias I couldn't find. The simplest fix: add `"ST"` as an alias     
   for `"STORE"` in gasm.rs's `is_valid_mnemonic()` and `emit_instruction()` match arms (2 lines of      
   Rust). That's the next step to get everything compiling.                                              
                                                                                                         
   ### What's not yet started:                                                                           
                                                                                                         
   - **Font glyph data table** at 0x410 (the 32 glyphs × 7 rows of 5-bit bitmaps are designed but not    
   added to the .gasm file)                                                                              
   - **8×8 zoom cell fill** with glyph overlay (the visual half of pixel fonts -- needs either           
   unrolled PSET loops or a clever register-saving approach)                                             
   - **The full self-authoring proof** -- paint a program that writes another program, run it            
                                                                                                         
   ### The path forward:                                                                                 
                                                                                                         
   1. Add `| "ST"` alias to `gasm.rs` (2 lines)

That would let you type `I` and get a 2-pixel sequence (opcode + data slot ready for +/- editing) instead of having to manually stamp each pixel. It would make building programs significantly faster since LDI is the most common instruction.
