The letter **X** in ASCII is decimal 88, which is **0x58** in hex.

But I realize there's a problem here. I listed X→MUL in the comments at the top of `text_key_handler` but never actually implemented the key check for it. X (88) falls through to `tkh_done` and does nothing.

If I add it, the chain would be:

```
LDI  r8, 88       ; 0x58
BEQ  r6, r8, tkh_mul
...
tkh_mul:  LDI  r9, 16   ; MUL = palette slot 16
          JMP  tkh_stamp
```

And the pixel that gets stamped would be the palette slot 16 instruction pixel: **0x00000007** -- opcode 7 (MUL), stratum 0, p1 0, p2 0.

Same issue for several other letters I listed in the doc but didn't wire up: F, G, O, Q, T, U, V, W, X, Y, Z. The doc shows 14 mappings but only 14 are implemented. Want me to add the missing ones?

● so we should make the letter x when pressed activate or be associated with the pixel value of 0x58 to

╭─ ⚕ Hermes ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────╮
Not exactly. The key code 0x58 and the instruction pixel value don't need to match. They serve different purposes:

- **0x58** is the ASCII code for the letter X -- it's what the keyboard sends when you press X
- The **instruction pixel** for MUL is 0x00000007 -- opcode 7, which the VM executes as multiplication

If you made the pixel value 0x58, the VM would try to execute opcode 88, which doesn't exist. The program wouldn't work.

But I think I see what you're getting at -- you want the letter and its pixel to be **self-consistent**, so that when you see "X" you know it IS pixel 0x58, and when the VM reads pixel 0x58 it knows it's "X". That's the pixel-font-as-encoding idea.

To make that work, you'd need to **assign opcodes to match ASCII letters**. So opcode 65 = A = ADD, opcode 68 = D = SUB, opcode 88 = X = MUL. The VM would use ASCII as its native instruction set. That's actually a cleaner design -- the character IS the opcode, no lookup table needed.

The tradeoff: you'd only have opcodes at ASCII letter positions (65-90, 97-122), which gives you about 52 possible instructions instead of 256. And you'd waste opcode space on non-letter ASCII values. But for a human-editable instruction set, it's elegant.

Is that the direction you want to go? It would mean redefining the VM's opcode table so that the key you press IS the byte the VM executes.
╰───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────╯
────────────────────────────────────────
● for a human-editable instruction set, it's elegant from a human point of view it seems obvious, but 
what do you think from your perspective?

────────────────────────────────────────


╭─ ⚕ Hermes ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────╮
Honestly? I think it's the right call, and I'll tell you why.

The current system has a mapping problem that I just spent a bunch of your time on. Opcode 5 is ADD, but A is 65. So I had to build a lookup table: key 65 → palette slot 3 → opcode 5. Three different numbers for the same concept. And I got the slot indices wrong twice. That's friction that doesn't need to exist.

If opcode 65 IS ADD, the whole chain collapses to one step: you press A, byte 0x41 goes into memory, VM reads 0x41, executes ADD. No lookup table. No palette slot indirection. No way to get the mapping wrong.

The practical benefits I can see:

**Pixel font rendering becomes free.** The opcode byte IS the ASCII character code. You want to render the glyph for an instruction? Just look up glyph[opcode]. Done. No slotmap, no colormap indirection for the character.

**You can read a hex dump as text.** Memory dump shows `41 00 00 00 41 03 2A 00 05 00 03 01 0D 00 00 00` and you can literally read "A...A.*.....M..." -- you see the A for ADD, the D I haven't mapped yet, the M for... wait, M would be 77. Point is, the printable letters jump out at you.

**The editor code gets simpler.** No text_key_handler with 14 BNE chains. Just: write the key code directly as the opcode byte. One instruction.

The cost is opcode space gets sparse -- gaps between letters. But you have 256 possible opcodes and only need ~30. Wasting 200 slots is fine. The VM's decoder doesn't care whether opcodes are at 0,1,2,3 or 65,66,67,68.

The one thing I'd watch: LDI still needs a data pixel after it. If LDI is 'I' (0x49), the next pixel contains the immediate value. That value can be anything -- it's data, not an opcode. So the value 88 for register loading would be byte 0x58, which happens to be 'X'. That's fine, it's in a data position, the VM treats it as a number not an instruction. But it could be confusing when reading dumps. Worth documenting that data pixels are not opcodes.

I'd do it. The current encoding is optimized for the machine. The ASCII encoding is optimized for the human who has to build and debug programs. Since the whole point of this system is that humans build by editing pixels, human-optimized is the right call.
